scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nationalgallery.org.uk/">www<wbr />.nationalgallery<wbr />.org<wbr />.uk</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>National Gallery</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Art_museum" title="Art museum">art museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> in the <a href="/wiki/City_of_Westminster" title="City of Westminster">City of Westminster</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Central_London" title="Central London">Central London</a>. Founded in 1824, it houses a collection of over 2,300 paintings dating from the mid-13th century to 1900.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Anone"><a href="#endnote_Anone">[a]</a></sup> \n</p><p>The Gallery is an <a href="/wiki/Exempt_charity" title="Exempt charity">exempt charity</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Non-departmental_public_body" title="Non-departmental public body">non-departmental public body</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Department_for_Culture,_Media_and_Sport" class="mw-redirect" title="Department for Culture, Media and Sport">Department for Culture, Media and Sport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Its collection belongs to the government on behalf of the British public, and entry to the main collection is free of charge. It is among the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_visited_art_museums_in_the_world" class="mw-redirect" title="List of most visited art museums in the world">most visited art museums</a> in the world, after the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-worldrank_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldrank-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Unlike comparable museums in continental Europe, the National Gallery was not formed by nationalising an existing royal or princely art collection. It came into being when the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">British government</a> bought 38 paintings from the heirs of <a href="/wiki/John_Julius_Angerstein" title="John Julius Angerstein">John Julius Angerstein</a> in 1824. After that initial purchase the Gallery was shaped mainly by its early directors, notably Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lock_Eastlake" title="Charles Lock Eastlake">Charles Lock Eastlake</a>, and by private donations, which today account for two-thirds of the collection.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The collection is small compared with many European national galleries, but encyclopaedic in scope; most major developments in Western painting "from <a href="/wiki/Giotto" title="Giotto">Giotto</a> to <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul C\xc3\xa9zanne">C\xc3\xa9zanne</a>"<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> are represented with important works. It used to be claimed that this was one of the few national galleries that had all its works on permanent exhibition,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> but this is no longer the case.\n</p><p>The present building, the third to house the National Gallery, was designed by <a href="/wiki/William_Wilkins_(architect)" title="William Wilkins (architect)">William Wilkins</a> from 1832 to 1838. Only the fa\xc3\xa7ade onto Trafalgar Square remains essentially unchanged from this time, as the building has been expanded piecemeal throughout its history. Wilkins\'s building was often criticised for the perceived weaknesses of its design and for its lack of space; the latter problem led to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Tate_Britain" title="Tate Britain">Tate Gallery</a> for British art in 1897. \n</p><p>The Sainsbury Wing, an extension to the west by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Venturi" title="Robert Venturi">Robert Venturi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denise_Scott_Brown" title="Denise Scott Brown">Denise Scott Brown</a>, is a notable example of <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodernist</a> architecture in Britain. The current Director of the National Gallery is <a href="/wiki/Gabriele_Finaldi" title="Gabriele Finaldi">Gabriele Finaldi</a>.\n</p><p>The Gallery was caught in controversy in 2018 over having some of the most expensive exhibition prices ever seen in London<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Call_for_a_National_Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Call for a National history</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Growth_under_Eastlake_and_his_successors"><span class="tocnumber">1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Growth under Eastlake and his successors</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Early_20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">1.4</span> <span class="toctext">Early 20th century</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#World_War_II"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">World War II</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Post-war_developments"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">Post-war developments</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Architecture"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Architecture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#William_Wilkins&#39;s_building"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">William Wilkins\'s building</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Alteration_and_expansion_(Pennethorne,_Barry_and_Taylor)"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Alteration and expansion (Pennethorne, Barry and Taylor)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#20th_century:_modernisation_versus_restoration"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">20th century: modernisation versus restoration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Sainsbury_Wing_and_later_additions"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Sainsbury Wing and later additions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Controversies"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Controversies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#List_of_Directors_of_the_National_Gallery"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">List of Directors of the National Gallery</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Collection_highlights"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Collection highlights</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Transport_connections"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Transport connections</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Call_for_a_National_Gallery">Call for a National Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Call for a National Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sebastiano_del_Piombo,_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Realistic painting of a robed figure, arms extended, standing outside on a small platform among people doing various things such as talking to each other, but most of whom are looking at him." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg/170px-Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg/255px-Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg/340px-Sebastiano_del_Piombo%2C_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2919" data-file-height="3874" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sebastiano_del_Piombo,_The_Raising_of_Lazarus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Raising_of_Lazarus_(Sebastiano_del_Piombo)" title="The Raising of Lazarus (Sebastiano del Piombo)">The Raising of Lazarus</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sebastiano_del_Piombo" title="Sebastiano del Piombo">Sebastiano del Piombo</a>, from the collection of <a href="/wiki/John_Julius_Angerstein" title="John Julius Angerstein">John Julius Angerstein</a>. This became the founding collection of the National Gallery in 1824. The painting has the <a href="/wiki/Accession_number_(library_science)" title="Accession number (library science)">accession number</a> NG1, making it officially the first painting to enter the Gallery.</div></div></div>\n<p>The late 18th century saw the nationalisation of royal or princely art collections across mainland Europe. The Bavarian royal collection (now in the <a href="/wiki/Alte_Pinakothek" title="Alte Pinakothek">Alte Pinakothek</a>, Munich) opened to the public in 1779, that of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Medici" title="House of Medici">Medici</a> in <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a> around 1789 (as the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi Gallery</a>), and the Museum Fran\xc3\xa7ais at the Louvre was formed out of the former French royal collection in 1793.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor1_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, however, did not emulate the continental model, and the British <a href="/wiki/Royal_Collection" title="Royal Collection">Royal Collection</a> remains in the sovereign\'s possession today. In 1777 the British government had the opportunity to buy an art collection of international stature, when the descendants of Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walpole" title="Robert Walpole">Robert Walpole</a> put his collection up for sale. The MP <a href="/wiki/John_Wilkes" title="John Wilkes">John Wilkes</a> argued for the government to buy this "invaluable treasure" and suggested that it be housed in "a noble gallery... to be built in the spacious garden of the British Museum"<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Nothing came of Wilkes\'s appeal and 20 years later the collection was bought in its entirety by <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine the Great</a>; it is now to be found in the <a href="/wiki/State_Hermitage_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="State Hermitage Museum">State Hermitage Museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/St_Petersburg" class="mw-redirect" title="St Petersburg">St Petersburg</a>.\n</p><p>A plan to acquire 150 paintings from the <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans_collection" class="mw-redirect" title="Orl\xc3\xa9ans collection">Orl\xc3\xa9ans collection</a>, which had been brought to London for sale in 1798, also failed, despite the interest of both the King and the Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/William_Pitt_the_Younger" title="William Pitt the Younger">Pitt the Younger</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The twenty-five paintings from that collection now in the Gallery, including "NG1", arrived later by a variety of routes. In 1799 the dealer Noel Desenfans offered a ready-made national collection to the British government; he and his partner Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bourgeois" title="Francis Bourgeois">Francis Bourgeois</a> had assembled it for the king of <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish\xe2\x80\x93Lithuanian Commonwealth">Poland</a>, before the <a href="/wiki/Third_Partition_of_Poland" title="Third Partition of Poland">Third Partition</a> in 1795 abolished Polish independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor1_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> This offer was declined and Bourgeois bequeathed the collection to his old school, <a href="/wiki/Dulwich_College" title="Dulwich College">Dulwich College</a>, on his death. The collection opened in 1814 in Britain\'s first purpose-built public gallery, the <a href="/wiki/Dulwich_Picture_Gallery" title="Dulwich Picture Gallery">Dulwich Picture Gallery</a>. The Scottish dealer William Buchanan and the collector Joseph Count Truchsess, both formed art collections expressly as the basis for a future national collection, but their respective offers (both made in 1803) were also declined.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor1_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the Walpole sale many artists, including <a href="/wiki/James_Barry_(painter)" title="James Barry (painter)">James Barry</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Flaxman" title="John Flaxman">John Flaxman</a>, had made renewed calls for the establishment of a National Gallery, arguing that a British school of painting could only flourish if it had access to the canon of European painting. The <a href="/wiki/British_Institution" title="British Institution">British Institution</a>, founded in 1805 by a group of aristocratic connoisseurs, attempted to address this situation. The members lent works to exhibitions that changed annually, while an art school was held in the summer months. However, as the paintings that were lent were often mediocre,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> some artists resented the Institution and saw it as a racket for the gentry to increase the sale prices of their Old Master paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> One of the Institution\'s founding members, Sir <a href="/wiki/Sir_George_Beaumont,_7th_Baronet" title="Sir George Beaumont, 7th Baronet">George Beaumont, Bt</a>, would eventually play a major role in the National Gallery\'s foundation by offering a gift of 16 paintings.\n</p><p>In 1823 another major art collection came on the market, which had been assembled by the recently deceased <a href="/wiki/John_Julius_Angerstein" title="John Julius Angerstein">John Julius Angerstein</a>. Angerstein was a Russian-born \xc3\xa9migr\xc3\xa9 banker based in London; his collection numbered 38 paintings, including works by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">Hogarth</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Marriage_%C3%A0-la-mode_(Hogarth)" class="mw-redirect" title="Marriage \xc3\xa0-la-mode (Hogarth)">Marriage \xc3\xa0-la-mode</a></i> series. On 1 July 1823 <a href="/wiki/George_Agar-Ellis,_1st_Baron_Dover" title="George Agar-Ellis, 1st Baron Dover">George Agar Ellis</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">Whig</a> politician, proposed to the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> that it purchase the collection.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The appeal was given added impetus by Beaumont\'s offer, which came with two conditions: that the government buy Angerstein\'s collection, and that a suitable building was to be found. The unexpected repayment of a war debt by <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> finally moved the government to buy Angerstein\'s collection, for \xc2\xa357,000.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation_and_early_history">Foundation and early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Foundation and early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Drawing of a three storied building, seen from the street. Women in long dresses date the picture." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg/170px-National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg" width="170" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg/255px-National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="321" data-file-height="450" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery_at_100_Pall_Mall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>100 <a href="/wiki/Pall_Mall,_London" title="Pall Mall, London">Pall Mall</a>, the home of the National Gallery from 1824 to 1834</div></div></div>\n<p>The National Gallery opened to the public on 10 May 1824, housed in Angerstein\'s former townhouse at No. 100 <a href="/wiki/Pall_Mall,_London" title="Pall Mall, London">Pall Mall</a>. Angerstein\'s paintings were joined in 1826 by those from Beaumont\'s collection, and in 1831 by the Reverend <a href="/wiki/William_Holwell_Carr" title="William Holwell Carr">William Holwell Carr</a>\'s bequest of 35 paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Initially the Keeper of Paintings, <a href="/wiki/William_Seguier" title="William Seguier">William Seguier</a>, bore the burden of managing the Gallery, but in July 1824 some of this responsibility fell to the newly formed board of trustees.\n</p><p>The National Gallery at <a href="/wiki/Pall_Mall,_London" title="Pall Mall, London">Pall Mall</a> was frequently overcrowded and hot and its diminutive size in comparison with the Louvre in Paris was a cause of national embarrassment. But Agar Ellis, by then a trustee of the Gallery, appraised the site for being "in the very gangway of London"; this was seen as necessary for the Gallery to fulfil its social purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor2_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Subsidence in No. 100 caused the Gallery to move briefly to No. 105 Pall Mall, which the novelist <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Trollope" title="Anthony Trollope">Anthony Trollope</a> described as a "dingy, dull, narrow house, ill-adapted for the exhibition of the treasures it held".<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor2_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> This in turn had to be demolished for the opening of a road to <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House_Terrace" title="Carlton House Terrace">Carlton House Terrace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Survey_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1832 construction began on a new building by <a href="/wiki/William_Wilkins_(architect)" title="William Wilkins (architect)">William Wilkins</a> on the site of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mews" title="Royal Mews">King\'s Mews</a> in <a href="/wiki/Charing_Cross" title="Charing Cross">Charing Cross</a>, in an area that had been transformed over the 1820s into <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a>. The location was a significant one, between the wealthy <a href="/wiki/West_End_of_London" title="West End of London">West End</a> and poorer areas to the east.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The argument that the collection could be accessed by people of all <a href="/wiki/Social_classes" class="mw-redirect" title="Social classes">social classes</a> outstripped other concerns, such as the pollution of central London or the failings of Wilkins\'s building, when the prospect of a move to <a href="/wiki/South_Kensington" title="South Kensington">South Kensington</a> was mooted in the 1850s. According to the Parliamentary Commission of 1857, "The <i>existence</i> of the pictures is not the end purpose of the collection, but the means only to give the people an ennobling enjoyment".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 1837 until 1868 the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> was housed in the east wing of the building.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_under_Eastlake_and_his_successors">Growth under Eastlake and his successors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Growth under Eastlake and his successors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>15th- and 16th-century Italian paintings were at the core of the National Gallery and for the first 30 years of its existence the Trustees\' independent acquisitions were mainly limited to works by <a href="/wiki/High_Renaissance" title="High Renaissance">High Renaissance</a> masters. Their conservative tastes resulted in several missed opportunities and the management of the Gallery later fell into complete disarray, with no acquisitions being made between 1847 and 1850.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A critical <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> report in 1851 called for the appointment of a director, whose authority would surpass that of the trustees. Many thought the position would go to the German <a href="/wiki/Art_historian" class="mw-redirect" title="Art historian">art historian</a> <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Friedrich_Waagen" title="Gustav Friedrich Waagen">Gustav Friedrich Waagen</a>, whom the Gallery had consulted on previous occasions about the lighting and display of the collections. However, the man preferred for the job by <a href="/wiki/Victoria_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Victoria of the United Kingdom">Queen Victoria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prince_Albert_of_Saxe-Coburg_and_Gotha" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Albert of Saxe-Coburg and Gotha">Prince Albert</a> and the Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Lord Russell</a>, was the Keeper of Paintings at the Gallery, Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lock_Eastlake" title="Charles Lock Eastlake">Charles Lock Eastlake</a>, who was President of the Royal Academy, played an essential role in the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Arundel_Society" title="Arundel Society">Arundel Society</a>, and knew most of London\'s leading art experts.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Piero,_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg/170px-Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg" width="170" height="248" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg/255px-Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg/340px-Piero%2C_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2893" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Piero,_battesimo_di_cristo_04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Baptism_of_Christ_(Piero_della_Francesca)" title="The Baptism of Christ (Piero della Francesca)">The Baptism of Christ</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Piero_della_Francesca" title="Piero della Francesca">Piero della Francesca</a>, one of Eastlake\'s purchases</div></div></div>\n<p>The new director\xe2\x80\x99s taste was for the <a href="/wiki/Early_Renaissance_painting" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Renaissance painting">Northern and Early Italian Renaissance</a> masters or "primitives", who had been neglected by the Gallery\xe2\x80\x99s acquisitions policy but were slowly gaining recognition from connoisseurs. Eastlake made annual tours to the continent and to Italy in particular, seeking out appropriate paintings to buy for the Gallery. In all, he bought 148 pictures abroad and 46 in Britain,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> among the former such seminal works as <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Uccello" title="Paolo Uccello">Paolo Uccello</a>\xe2\x80\x99s <i>Battle of San Romano</i>. Eastlake also amassed a private art collection during this period, consisting of paintings that he knew did not interest the trustees. His ultimate aim, however, was for them to enter the National Gallery; this was duly arranged upon his death by his friend and successor as director, <a href="/wiki/William_Boxall" title="William Boxall">William Boxall</a>, and his widow <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Eastlake" title="Elizabeth Eastlake">Lady Eastlake</a>.\n</p><p>The Gallery\xe2\x80\x99s lack of space remained acute in this period. In 1845 a large bequest of British paintings was made by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Vernon_(art_patron)" title="Robert Vernon (art patron)">Robert Vernon</a>; there was insufficient room in the Wilkins building so they were displayed first in Vernon\xe2\x80\x99s town house at No. 50 Pall Mall and then at <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_House" title="Marlborough House">Marlborough House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BakerHenry_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerHenry-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The Gallery was even less well equipped for its next major bequest, as <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a> was to bequeath the entire contents of his studio, excepting unfinished works, to the nation upon his death in 1851. The first 20 of these were displayed off-site in Marlborough House in 1856.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nicholson_Wornum" title="Ralph Nicholson Wornum">Ralph Nicholson Wornum</a> the Gallery\'s Keeper and Secretary, worked with <a href="/wiki/John_Ruskin" title="John Ruskin">John Ruskin</a> to bring the bequest together. The stipulation in Turner\'s will that two of his paintings be displayed alongside works by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Lorrain" title="Claude Lorrain">Claude</a><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> is still honoured in Room 15 of the Gallery, but his bequest has never been adequately displayed in its entirety; today the works are divided between Trafalgar Square and the Clore Gallery, a small purpose-built extension to the Tate completed in 1985.\n</p><p>The third director, Sir <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_Burton" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick William Burton">Frederick William Burton</a>, laid the foundations of the collection of 18th-century art and made several outstanding purchases from English private collections. The acquisition in 1885 of two paintings from <a href="/wiki/Blenheim_Palace" title="Blenheim Palace">Blenheim Palace</a>, Raphael\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Ansidei_Madonna" title="Ansidei Madonna">Ansidei Madonna</a></i> and Van Dyck\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Equestrian_Portrait_of_Charles_I" title="Equestrian Portrait of Charles I">Equestrian Portrait of Charles I</a></i>, with a record-setting grant of \xc2\xa387,500 from the Treasury, brought the Gallery\'s "golden age of collecting" to an end, as its annual purchase grant was suspended for several years thereafter.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When the Gallery purchased Holbein\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Ambassadors_(Holbein)" title="The Ambassadors (Holbein)">Ambassadors</a></i> from the <a href="/wiki/William_Pleydell-Bouverie,_5th_Earl_of_Radnor" title="William Pleydell-Bouverie, 5th Earl of Radnor">Earl of Radnor</a> in 1890, it did so with the aid of private individuals for the first time in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> In 1897 the formation of the National Gallery of British Art, known unofficially from early in its history as the <a href="/wiki/Tate" title="Tate">Tate Gallery</a>, allowed some British works to be moved off-site, following the precedent set by the Vernon collection and the Turner Bequest. Works by artists born after 1790 were moved to the new gallery on <a href="/wiki/Millbank" title="Millbank">Millbank</a>, which allowed <a href="/wiki/William_Hogarth" title="William Hogarth">Hogarth</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">Turner</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Constable" title="John Constable">Constable</a> to remain in Trafalgar Square.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RokebyVenus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Realistic painting of a nude woman seen from behind, reclining on a couch. She is looking at her reflection in a mirror held by a winged child." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/RokebyVenus.jpg/220px-RokebyVenus.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/RokebyVenus.jpg/330px-RokebyVenus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/RokebyVenus.jpg/440px-RokebyVenus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6162" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RokebyVenus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Venus at her Mirror</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Rokeby_Venus" class="mw-redirect" title="The Rokeby Venus">The Rokeby Venus</a></i>) by <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Vel\xc3\xa1zquez">Diego Vel\xc3\xa1zquez</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The agricultural crisis at the turn of the 20th century caused many aristocratic families to sell their paintings, but the British national collections were priced out of the market by American plutocrats.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> This prompted the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/The_Art_Fund" class="mw-redirect" title="The Art Fund">National Art Collections Fund</a>, a society of subscribers dedicated to stemming the flow of artworks to the United States. Their first acquisition for the National Gallery was <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Vel\xc3\xa1zquez">Vel\xc3\xa1zquez\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Rokeby_Venus" title="Rokeby Venus">Rokeby Venus</a></i> in 1906, followed by Holbein\'s <i>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Christina_of_Denmark" title="Christina of Denmark">Christina of Denmark</a></i> in 1909. However, despite the crisis in aristocratic fortunes, the following decade was one of several great bequests from private collectors. In 1909 the industrialist Dr <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mond" title="Ludwig Mond">Ludwig Mond</a> gave 42 Italian renaissance paintings, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Mond_Crucifixion" title="Mond Crucifixion">Mond Crucifixion</a></i> by Raphael, to the Gallery.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Other bequests of note were those of <a href="/wiki/George_Salting" title="George Salting">George Salting</a> in 1910, <a href="/wiki/Austen_Henry_Layard" title="Austen Henry Layard">Austen Henry Layard</a> in 1916 and Sir <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Lane" title="Hugh Lane">Hugh Lane</a> in 1917; the last of these was one of the Gallery\'s more controversial bequests.\n</p><p>In a rare example of the political protest for which Trafalgar Square is famous occurring in the National Gallery, the <i>Rokeby Venus</i> was damaged on 10 March 1914 by <a href="/wiki/Mary_Richardson" title="Mary Richardson">Mary Richardson</a>, a campaigner for <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women&#39;s suffrage">women\'s suffrage</a>, in protest against the arrest of <a href="/wiki/Emmeline_Pankhurst" title="Emmeline Pankhurst">Emmeline Pankhurst</a> the previous day. Later that month another suffragette attacked five <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Bellini" title="Giovanni Bellini">Bellinis</a>, causing the Gallery to close until the start of the <a href="/w/index.php?title=First_World_War,&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="First World War, (page does not exist)">First World War,</a> when the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Social_and_Political_Union" title="Women&#39;s Social and Political Union">Women\'s Social and Political Union</a> called for an end to violent acts drawing attention to their plight.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The reception of Impressionist art at the Gallery got off to an exceptionally stormy start. In 1906, Sir Hugh Lane promised 39 paintings, including <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Auguste_Renoir" title="Pierre-Auguste Renoir">Renoir</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Umbrellas_(Renoir_painting)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Umbrellas (Renoir painting)">Umbrellas</a></i>, to the National Gallery on his death, unless a suitable building could be built in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>. Although eagerly accepted by the director <a href="/wiki/Charles_Holroyd" title="Charles Holroyd">Charles Holroyd</a>, they were received with extreme hostility by the Trustees; <a href="/wiki/Algernon_Freeman-Mitford,_1st_Baron_Redesdale" title="Algernon Freeman-Mitford, 1st Baron Redesdale">Lord Redesdale</a> wrote that "I would as soon expect to hear of a Mormon service being conducted in St. Paul\'s Cathedral as to see the exhibition of the works of the modern French Art-rebels in the sacred precincts of Trafalgar Square".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Perhaps as a result of such attitudes, Lane amended his will with a codicil that the works should only go to Ireland, but crucially this was never witnessed.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Lane died on board the <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">RMS Lusitania</a></i> in 1915, and a dispute began which was not resolved until 1959. Part of the collection is now on permanent loan to <a href="/wiki/Dublin_City_Gallery_The_Hugh_Lane" class="mw-redirect" title="Dublin City Gallery The Hugh Lane">Dublin City Gallery ("The Hugh Lane")</a> and other works rotate between London and Dublin every few years.\n</p><p>A fund for the purchase of modern paintings established by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Courtauld_(art_collector)" title="Samuel Courtauld (art collector)">Samuel Courtauld</a> in 1923 bought <a href="/wiki/Georges-Pierre_Seurat" class="mw-redirect" title="Georges-Pierre Seurat">Seurat\'s</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Bathers_at_Asni%C3%A8res" title="Bathers at Asni\xc3\xa8res">Bathers at Asni\xc3\xa8res</a></i> and other notable modern works for the nation;<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> in 1934 these transferred to the National Gallery from the Tate.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg/220px-The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg/330px-The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg/440px-The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="573" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Evacuation_of_Paintings_From_London_during_the_Second_World_War_HU36302.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paintings being evacuated from the National Gallery during the Second World War</div></div></div>\n<p>Shortly before the outbreak of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> the paintings were evacuated to various locations in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Penrhyn_Castle" title="Penrhyn Castle">Penrhyn Castle</a>, the university colleges of <a href="/wiki/Bangor_University" title="Bangor University">Bangor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aberystwyth_University" title="Aberystwyth University">Aberystwyth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In 1940, during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_France" title="Battle of France">Battle of France</a>, a more secure home was sought, and there were discussions about moving the paintings to Canada. This idea was firmly rejected by <a href="/wiki/Winston_Churchill" title="Winston Churchill">Winston Churchill</a>, who wrote in a telegram to the director <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Clark" title="Kenneth Clark">Kenneth Clark</a>, \xe2\x80\x9cbury them in caves or in cellars, but not a picture shall leave these islands\xe2\x80\x9d.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Instead a slate quarry at Manod, near <a href="/wiki/Blaenau_Ffestiniog" title="Blaenau Ffestiniog">Blaenau Ffestiniog</a> in North Wales, was requisitioned for the Gallery\'s use. In the seclusion afforded by the paintings\' new location, the Keeper (and future director) <a href="/wiki/Martin_Davies_(museum_director)" title="Martin Davies (museum director)">Martin Davies</a> began to compile scholarly catalogues on the collection, helped by the fact that the Gallery\'s library was also stored in the quarry. The move to Manod confirmed the importance of storing paintings at a constant temperature and humidity, something the Gallery\'s conservators had long suspected but had hitherto been unable to prove.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> This eventually resulted in the first air-conditioned gallery opening in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-BakerHenry_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerHenry-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the course of the war <a href="/wiki/Myra_Hess" title="Myra Hess">Myra Hess</a>, and other musicians, such as <a href="/wiki/Moura_Lympany" title="Moura Lympany">Moura Lympany</a>, gave daily lunch-time recitals in the empty building, to raise public morale at a time when every concert hall in London was closed.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> A number of art exhibitions were held at the Gallery as a complement to the recitals. The first of these was <i>British Painting since Whistler</i> in 1940, organised by <a href="/wiki/Lillian_Browse" title="Lillian Browse">Lillian Browse</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-farr_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-farr-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> who also mounted the major joint retrospective <i>Exhibition of Paintings by <a href="/wiki/William_Nicholson_(artist)" title="William Nicholson (artist)">Sir William Nicholson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jack_B._Yeats" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack B. Yeats">Jack B. Yeats</a></i> held from 1 January \xe2\x80\x93 15 March 1942, which was seen by 10,518 visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-1942cat_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1942cat-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-reed_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reed-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Exhibitions of work by war artists, including <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nash_(artist)" title="Paul Nash (artist)">Paul Nash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Moore" title="Henry Moore">Henry Moore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Spencer" title="Stanley Spencer">Stanley Spencer</a>, were also held; the <a href="/wiki/War_Artists%27_Advisory_Committee" title="War Artists&#39; Advisory Committee">War Artists\' Advisory Committee</a> had been set up by Clark in order "to keep artists at work on any pretext".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 1941 a request from an artist to see <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">Rembrandt</a>\'s <i>Portrait of Margaretha de Geer</i> (a new acquisition) resulted in the "Picture of the Month" scheme, in which a single painting was removed from Manod and exhibited to the general public in the National Gallery each month. The art critic <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Read" title="Herbert Read">Herbert Read</a>, writing that year, called the National Gallery "a defiant outpost of culture right in the middle of a bombed and shattered metropolis".<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The paintings returned to Trafalgar Square in 1945.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_developments">Post-war developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-war developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:234px;max-width:234px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of a man happening upon a group of nude women, bathing in a grotto-like space." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/230px-Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="230" height="211" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/345px-Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/460px-Titian_-_Diana_and_Actaeon_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2750" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Diana_and_Actaeon_(Titian)" title="Diana and Actaeon (Titian)">Diana and Actaeon</a></i>, bought in 2008, jointly with the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="National Gallery of Scotland">National Gallery of Scotland</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Painting of two groups of mostly nude women; on the right, the goddess Diana points accusingly at a woman in the left group who lies on the floor in a state of distress." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg/230px-TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg" width="230" height="212" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg/345px-TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg/460px-TitianDianaCallistoEdinburgh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4579" data-file-height="4226" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Titian\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Diana_and_Callisto" title="Diana and Callisto">Diana and Callisto</a></i>, bought in 2012, jointly with the National Gallery of Scotland</div></div></div></div>\n<p>In the post-war years, acquisitions have become increasingly difficult for the National Gallery as the prices for <a href="/wiki/Old_Masters" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Masters">Old Masters</a> \xe2\x80\x93 and even more so for the <a href="/wiki/Impressionists" class="mw-redirect" title="Impressionists">Impressionists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-impressionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-impressionism">Post-impressionists</a> \xe2\x80\x93 have risen beyond its means. Some of the Gallery\'s most significant purchases in this period would have been impossible without the major public appeals backing them, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Virgin_and_Child_with_St._Anne_and_St._John_the_Baptist" class="mw-redirect" title="The Virgin and Child with St. Anne and St. John the Baptist">The Virgin and Child with St. Anne and St. John the Baptist</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci" title="Leonardo da Vinci">Leonardo da Vinci</a> (bought in 1962) and <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Death_of_Actaeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Death of Actaeon">Death of Actaeon</a></i> (1972). The Gallery\'s purchase grant from the government was frozen in 1985, but later that year it received an endowment of \xc2\xa350 million from Sir <a href="/wiki/Paul_Getty" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul Getty">Paul Getty</a>, enabling many major purchases to be made.<sup id="cite_ref-BakerHenry_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BakerHenry-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In April 1985 <a href="/wiki/John_Sainsbury,_Baron_Sainsbury_of_Preston_Candover" title="John Sainsbury, Baron Sainsbury of Preston Candover">Lord Sainsbury of Preston Candover</a> and his brothers, the Hon. <a href="/wiki/Simon_Sainsbury" title="Simon Sainsbury">Simon Sainsbury</a> and Sir <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Sainsbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Timothy Sainsbury">Timothy Sainsbury</a>, had made a donation that would enable the construction of the Sainsbury Wing.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The directorship of <a href="/wiki/Neil_MacGregor" title="Neil MacGregor">Neil MacGregor</a> saw a major rehang at the Gallery, dispensing with the classification of paintings by national school that had been introduced by Eastlake. The new chronological hang sought to emphasise the interaction between cultures rather than fixed national characteristics, reflecting the change in art historical values since the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In other respects, however, Victorian tastes were rehabilitated: the building\'s interiors were no longer considered an embarrassment and were restored, and in 1999 the Gallery accepted a bequest of 26 <a href="/wiki/Italian_Baroque" title="Italian Baroque">Italian Baroque</a> paintings from Sir <a href="/wiki/Denis_Mahon" title="Denis Mahon">Denis Mahon</a>. Earlier in the 20th century many considered the Baroque to be beyond the pale: in 1945 the Gallery\'s trustees declined to buy a <a href="/wiki/Guercino" title="Guercino">Guercino</a> from Mahon\'s collection for \xc2\xa3200. The same painting was valued at \xc2\xa34&#160;million in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Mahon\'s bequest was made on the condition that the Gallery would never <a href="/wiki/Collection_(museum)#Deaccessioning" class="mw-redirect" title="Collection (museum)">deaccession</a> any of its paintings or charge for admission.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaskell_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskell-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="float:left; margin:auto; margin:1em 1em 2em 0; font-size:95%;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2">Associate artists\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Paula_Rego" title="Paula Rego">Paula Rego</a></td>\n<td>1989\xe2\x80\x931990\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ken_Kiff" title="Ken Kiff">Ken Kiff</a></td>\n<td>1991\xe2\x80\x931993\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Peter_Blake_(artist)" title="Peter Blake (artist)">Peter Blake</a></td>\n<td>1994\xe2\x80\x931996\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ana_Maria_Pacheco" title="Ana Maria Pacheco">Ana Maria Pacheco</a></td>\n<td>1997\xe2\x80\x931999\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ron_Mueck" title="Ron Mueck">Ron Mueck</a></td>\n<td>2000\xe2\x80\x932002\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/John_Virtue" title="John Virtue">John Virtue</a></td>\n<td>2003\xe2\x80\x932005\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Alison_Watt_(Scottish_painter)" title="Alison Watt (Scottish painter)">Alison Watt</a></td>\n<td>2006\xe2\x80\x932008\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Michael_Landy" title="Michael Landy">Michael Landy</a></td>\n<td>2009\xe2\x80\x932013\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/George_Shaw_(artist)" title="George Shaw (artist)">George Shaw</a></td>\n<td>2014\xe2\x80\x93\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Since 1989, the gallery has run a scheme that gives a studio to contemporary artists to create work based on the permanent collection. They usually hold the position of associate artist for two years and are given an exhibition in the National Gallery at the end of their tenure.\n</p><p>The respective remits of the National and Tate Galleries, which had long been contested by the two institutions, were more clearly defined in 1996. 1900 was established as the cut-off point for paintings in the National Gallery, and in 1997 more than 60 post-1900 paintings from the collection were given to the Tate on a long-term loan, in return for works by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gauguin" title="Paul Gauguin">Gauguin</a> and others. However, future expansion of the National Gallery may yet see the return of 20th-century paintings to its walls.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 21st century there have been three large fundraising campaigns at the Gallery: in 2004, to buy Raphael\xe2\x80\x99s <i><a href="/wiki/Madonna_of_the_Pinks" title="Madonna of the Pinks">Madonna of the Pinks</a></i>, in 2008, for Titian\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Diana_and_Actaeon_(Titian)" title="Diana and Actaeon (Titian)">Diana and Actaeon</a></i>, and in 2012, Titian\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Diana_and_Callisto" title="Diana and Callisto">Diana and Callisto</a></i>. Both Titians were bought in tandem with the <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="National Gallery of Scotland">National Gallery of Scotland</a> for \xc2\xa395&#160;m. Both of these major works were sold from the famous <a href="/wiki/Bridgewater_Loan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bridgewater Loan">collection of the Duke of Sutherland</a>. The National Gallery is now largely priced out of the market for Old Master paintings and can only make such acquisitions with the backing of major public appeals; the departing director <a href="/wiki/Charles_Saumarez_Smith" title="Charles Saumarez Smith">Charles Saumarez Smith</a> expressed his frustration at this situation in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-gayford_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gayford-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2014, the National Gallery was the subject of a documentary film by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Wiseman" title="Frederick Wiseman">Frederick Wiseman</a>. The film shows the gallery administration and staff at work, the conservation laboratory, docent tours, and the mounting of <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_Da_Vinci" class="mw-redirect" title="Leonardo Da Vinci">Leonardo Da Vinci</a>, <a href="/wiki/J._M._W._Turner" title="J. M. W. Turner">J. M. W. Turner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a> exhibitions in 2011\xe2\x80\x932012.\n<sup id="cite_ref-dargis_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dargis-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="William_Wilkins.27s_building"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="William_Wilkins\'s_building">William Wilkins\'s building</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: William Wilkins&#039;s building">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em;width:250px"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="fn org" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:120%; text-align:center">National Gallery</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG" class="image"><img alt="William Wilkins&#39;s building.JPG" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG/250px-William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG" width="250" height="188" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG/375px-William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG/500px-William_Wilkins%27s_building.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a><div>The Wilkins Building, with the church of <a href="/wiki/St_Martin-in-the-Fields" title="St Martin-in-the-Fields">St Martin-in-the-Fields</a> to the right</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Built</th><td>\n1832\xe2\x80\x931838</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Architect</th><td>\nWilliam Wilkins</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Architectural style(s)</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">Neoclassical</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div style="border:4px solid #FFC0CB; line-height: 1.5; text-align: center;">\n<a href="/wiki/Listed_building#England_and_Wales" title="Listed building">Listed Building</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Grade I</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<i>Official name: National Gallery</i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Designated</th><td>\n5 February 1970</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Reference&#160;no.</th><td>\n1066236<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The first suggestion for a National Gallery on Trafalgar Square came from <a href="/wiki/John_Nash_(architect)" title="John Nash (architect)">John Nash</a>, who envisaged it on the site of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mews" title="Royal Mews">King\'s Mews</a>, while a <a href="/wiki/Parthenon" title="Parthenon">Parthenon</a>-like building for the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> would occupy the centre of the square.<sup id="cite_ref-Liscombe_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liscombe-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Economic recession prevented this scheme from being built, but a competition for the Mews site was eventually held in 1831, for which Nash submitted a design with <a href="/wiki/Charles_Robert_Cockerell" title="Charles Robert Cockerell">C. R. Cockerell</a> as his co-architect. Nash\'s popularity was waning by this time, however, and the commission was awarded to <a href="/wiki/William_Wilkins_(architect)" title="William Wilkins (architect)">William Wilkins</a>, who was involved in the selection of the site and submitted some drawings at the last moment.<sup id="cite_ref-Summerson_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summerson-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Wilkins had hoped to build a "Temple of the Arts, nurturing contemporary art through historical example",<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> but the commission was blighted by parsimony and compromise, and the resulting building was deemed a failure on almost all counts.\n</p><p>The site only allowed for the building to be one room deep, as a workhouse and a barracks lay immediately behind.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Bnone"><a href="#endnote_Bnone">[b]</a></sup> To exacerbate matters, there was a public right of way through the site to these buildings, which accounts for the access porticoes on the eastern and western sides of the fa\xc3\xa7ade. These had to incorporate columns from the demolished <a href="/wiki/Carlton_House,_London" class="mw-redirect" title="Carlton House, London">Carlton House</a> and their relative shortness result in an elevation that was deemed excessively low, and a far cry from the commanding focal point that was desired for the northern end of the Square. Also recycled are the sculptures on the fa\xc3\xa7ade, originally intended for Nash\'s <a href="/wiki/Marble_Arch" title="Marble Arch">Marble Arch</a> but abandoned due to his financial problems.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Cnone"><a href="#endnote_Cnone">[c]</a></sup> The eastern half of the building housed the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> until 1868, which further diminished the space afforded to the Gallery.\n</p><p>The building was the object of public ridicule before it had even been completed, as a version of the design had been leaked to the <i>Literary Gazette</i> in 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\nTwo years before completion, its infamous "pepperpot" elevation appeared on the frontispiece of <i>Contrasts</i> (1836), an influential tract by the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">Gothicist</a> <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Pugin" title="Augustus Pugin">A. W. N. Pugin</a>, as an example of the degeneracy of the classical style.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/William_IV_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="William IV of the United Kingdom">William IV</a> (in his last recorded utterance) thought the building a "nasty little pokey hole",<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_50_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_50-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/William_Makepeace_Thackeray" title="William Makepeace Thackeray">William Makepeace Thackeray</a> called it "a little gin shop of a building".<sup id="cite_ref-Smith_50_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_50-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The twentieth-century architectural historian Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Summerson" title="John Summerson">John Summerson</a> echoed these early criticisms when he compared the arrangement of a <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">dome</a> and two diminutive <a href="/wiki/Turret" title="Turret">turrets</a> on the roofline to "the clock and vases on a mantelpiece, only less useful".<sup id="cite_ref-Summerson_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Summerson-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a>\'s landscaping of Trafalgar Square, from 1840, included a north terrace so that the building would appear to be raised, thus addressing one of the points of complaint.<sup id="cite_ref-Survey_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Survey-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Opinion on the building had mellowed considerably by 1984, when the <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> called the Wilkins fa\xc3\xa7ade a "much-loved and elegant friend", in contrast to a proposed extension. (<i><a href="#Sainsbury_Wing_and_later_additions">See below</a></i>)\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 352px"><div style="width: 352px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 350px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg/525px-National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg" width="350" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg/788px-National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg/1050px-National_Gallery_London_2013_March.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1280" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The elevation onto Trafalgar Square in 2013\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 287.33333333333px"><div style="width: 287.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 285.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery1836.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/National_Gallery1836.jpg/428px-National_Gallery1836.jpg" width="286" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/National_Gallery1836.jpg/644px-National_Gallery1836.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/National_Gallery1836.jpg 2x" data-file-width="712" data-file-height="436" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The <i>piano nobile</i> and ground floor of Wilkins\'s building, before expansion. Note the passageways behind the east and west porticoes. Areas shaded in pink were used by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Academy" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Academy">Royal Academy</a> until 1868.\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 221.33333333333px"><div style="width: 221.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 219.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:-0.16666666666667px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg/329px-National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg.png" width="220" height="176" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg/495px-National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg/660px-National_Gallery_1st_floor_plan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="592" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Plan of the first floor of the National Gallery in 2013\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span id="Alteration_and_expansion_.28Pennethorne.2C_Barry_and_Taylor.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Alteration_and_expansion_(Pennethorne,_Barry_and_Taylor)">Alteration and expansion (Pennethorne, Barry and Taylor)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Alteration and expansion (Pennethorne, Barry and Taylor)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first significant alteration made to the building was the single, long gallery added by Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Pennethorne" title="James Pennethorne">James Pennethorne</a> in 1860\xe2\x80\x931. Ornately decorated in comparison with the rooms by Wilkins, it nonetheless worsened the cramped conditions inside the building as it was built over the original entrance hall.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Unsurprisingly, several attempts were made either to completely remodel the National Gallery (as suggested by Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Barry" title="Charles Barry">Charles Barry</a> in 1853), or to move it to more capacious premises in <a href="/wiki/Kensington" title="Kensington">Kensington</a>, where the air was also cleaner. In 1867 Barry\'s son <a href="/wiki/Edward_Middleton_Barry" title="Edward Middleton Barry">Edward Middleton Barry</a> proposed to replace the Wilkins building with a massive classical building with four domes. The scheme was a failure and contemporary critics denounced the exterior as "a strong plagiarism upon <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul&#39;s Cathedral">St Paul\'s Cathedral</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the demolition of the workhouse, however, Barry was able to build the Gallery\'s first sequence of grand architectural spaces, from 1872 to 1876. Built to a polychrome <a href="/wiki/Neo-Renaissance" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Renaissance">Neo-Renaissance</a> design, the Barry Rooms were arranged on a <a href="/wiki/Greek_cross" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek cross">Greek cross</a>-plan around a huge central octagon. Though it compensated for the underwhelming architecture of the Wilkins building, Barry\'s new wing was disliked by Gallery staff, who considered its monumental aspect to be in conflict with its function as exhibition space. Also, the decorative programme of the rooms did not take their intended contents into account; the ceiling of the 15th- and 16th-century Italian gallery, for instance, was inscribed with the names of British artists of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> But despite these failures, the Barry Rooms provided the Gallery with a strong axial groundplan. This was to be followed by all subsequent additions to the Gallery for a century, resulting in a building of clear symmetry.\n</p><p>Pennethorne\'s gallery was demolished for the next phase of building, a scheme by Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Taylor_(architect)" title="John Taylor (architect)">John Taylor</a> extending northwards of the main entrance. Its glass-domed entrance vestibule had painted ceiling decorations by the <a href="/wiki/John_Dibblee_Crace" title="John Dibblee Crace">Crace</a> family firm, who had also worked on the Barry Rooms. A fresco intended for the south wall was never realised, and that space is now taken up by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_Leighton,_1st_Baron_Leighton" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederic Leighton, 1st Baron Leighton">Frederic, Lord Leighton</a>\xe2\x80\x99s painting of <i><a href="/wiki/Cimabue%27s_Celebrated_Madonna" title="Cimabue&#39;s Celebrated Madonna">Cimabue\'s Celebrated Madonna carried in Procession through the Streets of Florence</a></i> (1853\xe2\x80\x9355), lent by the Royal Collection in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 128px"><div style="width: 128px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb"
