2x" data-file-width="3599" data-file-height="3593" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>The dome of the Staircase Hall\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century:_modernisation_versus_restoration">20th century: modernisation versus restoration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 20th century: modernisation versus restoration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boris_Anrep_mosaic,_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg/170px-Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg/255px-Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg/340px-Boris_Anrep_mosaic%2C_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boris_Anrep_mosaic,_The_National_Gallery_-_The_Awakening_of_the_Muses.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Boris_Anrep#National_Gallery_mosaics_(1928\xe2\x80\x9352)" title="Boris Anrep">The Awakening of the Muses</a></i> (1933), a mosaic by Boris Anrep.</div></div></div>\n<p>Later additions to the west came more steadily but maintained the coherence of the building by mirroring Barry\'s cross-axis plan to the east. The use of dark marble for doorcases was also continued, giving the extensions a degree of internal consistency with the older rooms. The classical style was still in use at the National Gallery in 1929, when a <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts</a> style gallery was built, funded by the art dealer and Trustee <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Duveen,_1st_Baron_Duveen_of_Millbank" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Duveen, 1st Baron Duveen of Millbank">Lord Duveen</a>. However, it was not long before the 20th-century reaction against Victorian attitudes became manifest at the Gallery. From 1928 to 1952 the landing floors of Taylor\'s entrance hall were relaid with a new series of <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaics</a> by <a href="/wiki/Boris_Anrep" title="Boris Anrep">Boris Anrep</a>, who was friendly with the <a href="/wiki/Bloomsbury_Group" title="Bloomsbury Group">Bloomsbury Group</a>. These mosaics can be read as a satire on 19th-century conventions for the decoration of public buildings, as typified by the <a href="/wiki/Albert_Memorial" title="Albert Memorial">Albert Memorial</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Frieze_of_Parnassus" title="Frieze of Parnassus">Frieze of Parnassus</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The central mosaic depicting <i>The Awakening of the Muses</i> includes portraits of <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf">Virginia Woolf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greta_Garbo" title="Greta Garbo">Greta Garbo</a>, subverting the high moral tone of its Victorian forebears. In place of Christianity\'s seven virtues, Anrep offered his own set of <i>Modern Virtues</i>, including "Humour" and "Open Mind"; the allegorical figures are again portraits of his contemporaries, including Winston Churchill, <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> and <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 20th century the Gallery\'s late Victorian interiors fell out of fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> The Crace ceiling decorations in the entrance hall were not to the taste of the director <a href="/wiki/Charles_Holmes" title="Charles Holmes">Charles Holmes</a>, and were obliterated by white paint.<sup id="cite_ref-Jury2007_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jury2007-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\nThe North Galleries, which opened to the public in 1975, marked the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modernist architecture</a> at the National Gallery. In the older rooms, the original classical details were effaced by partitions, daises and suspended ceilings, the aim being to create neutral settings which did not distract from contemplation of the paintings. But the Gallery\'s commitment to modernism was short-lived: by the 1980s Victorian style was no longer considered anathema, and a restoration programme began to restore the 19th- and early-20th-century interiors to their purported original appearance. This began with the refurbishment of the Barry Rooms in 1985\xe2\x80\x936. From 1996 to 1999 even the North Galleries, by then considered to "lack a positive architectural character" were remodelled in a classical style, albeit a simplified one.<sup id="cite_ref-Gaskell_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gaskell-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sainsbury_Wing_and_later_additions">Sainsbury Wing and later additions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sainsbury Wing and later additions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em;width:250px"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="fn org" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size:120%; text-align:center">Sainsbury Wing</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<a href="/wiki/File:National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg" class="image"><img alt="National Gallery London Sainsbury Wing 2006-04-17.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg/250px-National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg" width="250" height="188" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg/375px-National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg/500px-National_Gallery_London_Sainsbury_Wing_2006-04-17.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a><div>The Sainsbury Wing, as built, seen from Trafalgar Square</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Built</th><td>\n1988\xe2\x80\x931991</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Architect</th><td>\nRobert Venturi, Denise Scott Brown and Associates</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:bold">Architectural style(s)</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodernist</a></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div style="border:4px solid #FFC0CB; line-height: 1.5; text-align: center;">\n<a href="/wiki/Listed_building#England_and_Wales" title="Listed building">Listed Building</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Grade I</div></th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">\n<i>Official name: Sainsbury Wing at the National&#160;Gallery</i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Designated</th><td>\n9 May 2018</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Reference&#160;no.</th><td>\n1451082<sup id="cite_ref-NHLE_Sainsbury_Wing_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHLE_Sainsbury_Wing-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The most important addition to the building in recent years has been the Sainsbury Wing, designed by the postmodernist architects <a href="/wiki/Robert_Venturi" title="Robert Venturi">Robert Venturi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denise_Scott_Brown" title="Denise Scott Brown">Denise Scott Brown</a> to house the collection of Renaissance paintings, and built in 1991. The building occupies the "Hampton\'s site" to the west of the main building, where a department store of the same name had stood until its destruction in the <a href="/wiki/The_Blitz" title="The Blitz">Blitz</a>. The Gallery had long sought expansion into this space<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and in 1982 a competition was held to find a suitable architect; the shortlist included a radical <a href="/wiki/High-tech_architecture" title="High-tech architecture">high-tech</a> proposal by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rogers" title="Richard Rogers">Richard Rogers</a>, among others. The design that won the most votes was by the firm <a href="/wiki/Ahrends,_Burton_and_Koralek" title="Ahrends, Burton and Koralek">Ahrends, Burton and Koralek</a>, who then modified their proposal to include a tower, similar to that of the Rogers scheme. The proposal was dropped after the <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a> compared the design to a "monstrous <a href="/wiki/Carbuncle" title="Carbuncle">carbuncle</a> on the face of a much-loved and elegant friend",<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The term "monstrous carbuncle", for a modern building that clashes with its surroundings, has since become commonplace.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>One of the conditions of the 1982 competition was that the new wing had to include commercial offices as well as public gallery space. However, in 1985 it became possible to devote the extension entirely to the Gallery\'s uses, due to a donation of almost \xc2\xa350 million from <a href="/wiki/John_Sainsbury,_Baron_Sainsbury_of_Preston_Candover" title="John Sainsbury, Baron Sainsbury of Preston Candover">Lord Sainsbury</a> and his brothers <a href="/wiki/Simon_Sainsbury" title="Simon Sainsbury">Simon</a> and Sir <a href="/wiki/Tim_Sainsbury" title="Tim Sainsbury">Tim Sainsbury</a>. A closed competition was held, and the schemes produced were noticeably more restrained than in the earlier competition.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional,_Londres,_Inglaterra,_2014-08-11,_DD_173.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG/220px-Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG" width="220" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG/330px-Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG/440px-Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional%2C_Londres%2C_Inglaterra%2C_2014-08-11%2C_DD_173.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3107" data-file-height="3203" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galer%C3%ADa_Nacional,_Londres,_Inglaterra,_2014-08-11,_DD_173.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The main <a href="/wiki/Enfilade_(architecture)" title="Enfilade (architecture)">enfilade</a> of the Sainsbury Wing</div></div></div>\n<p>In contrast with the rich ornamentation of the main building, the galleries in the Sainsbury Wing are pared-down and intimate, to suit the smaller scale of many of the paintings<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. The main inspirations for these rooms are Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Soane" title="John Soane">John Soane</a>\'s toplit galleries for the <a href="/wiki/Dulwich_Picture_Gallery" title="Dulwich Picture Gallery">Dulwich Picture Gallery</a> and the church interiors of <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Brunelleschi" title="Filippo Brunelleschi">Filippo Brunelleschi</a> (the stone dressing is in <i>pietra serena</i>, the grey stone local to Florence)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. The northernmost galleries align with Barry\'s central axis, so that there is a single vista down the whole length of the Gallery. This axis is exaggerated by the use of <a href="/wiki/False_perspective" class="mw-redirect" title="False perspective">false perspective</a>, as the columns flanking each opening gradually diminish in size until the visitor reaches the focal point (as of 2009), an altarpiece by <a href="/wiki/Cima_da_Conegliano" title="Cima da Conegliano">Cima</a> of <i>The Incredulity of St Thomas</i>. Venturi\'s postmodernist approach to architecture is in full evidence at the Sainsbury Wing, with its stylistic quotations from buildings as disparate as the clubhouses on Pall Mall, the <a href="/wiki/Scala_Regia_(Vatican)" title="Scala Regia (Vatican)">Scala Regia</a> in the Vatican, Victorian warehouses and Ancient Egyptian temples.\n</p><p>Following the pedestrianisation of Trafalgar Square, the Gallery is currently engaged in a masterplan to convert the vacated office space on the ground floor into public space. The plan will also fill in disused courtyards and make use of land acquired from the adjoining <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery_(London)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Portrait Gallery (London)">National Portrait Gallery</a> in St Martin\'s Place, which it gave to the National Gallery in exchange for land for its 2000 extension. The first phase, the East Wing Project designed by Jeremy Dixon and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jones_(English_architect)" title="Edward Jones (English architect)">Edward Jones</a>, opened to the public in 2004. This provided a new ground level entrance from Trafalgar Square, named in honour of Sir <a href="/wiki/Paul_Getty" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul Getty">Paul Getty</a>. The main entrance was also refurbished, and reopened in September 2005. Possible future projects include a "West Wing Project" roughly symmetrical with the East Wing Project, which would provide a future ground level entrance, and the public opening of some small rooms at the far eastern end of the building acquired as part of the swap with the National Portrait Gallery. This might include a new public staircase in the bow on the eastern fa\xc3\xa7ade. No timetable has been announced for these additional projects.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/220px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg" width="220" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/330px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg/440px-Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4670" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Titian_Bacchus_and_Ariadne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The restoration of <a href="/wiki/Titian" title="Titian">Titian</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Bacchus_and_Ariadne" title="Bacchus and Ariadne">Bacchus and Ariadne</a></i> from 1967 to 1968 was a controversial restoration at the National Gallery, due to concern that the painting\'s <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/tonality" class="extiw" title="wikt:tonality">tonality</a> had been thrown out of balance.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>One of the most persistent criticisms of the National Gallery, apart from those who criticise inadequacies of the building, has been of its conservation policy. The Gallery\'s detractors accused it of having had an over-zealous approach to restoration. The first cleaning operation at the National Gallery began in 1844 after Eastlake\'s appointment as Keeper, and was the subject of attacks in the press after the first three paintings to receive the treatment \xe2\x80\x93 a <a href="/wiki/Peter_Paul_Rubens" title="Peter Paul Rubens">Rubens</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Aelbert_Cuyp" title="Aelbert Cuyp">Cuyp</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez" title="Diego Vel\xc3\xa1zquez">Vel\xc3\xa1zquez</a> \xe2\x80\x93 were unveiled to the public in 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> The Gallery\'s most virulent critic was J. Morris Moore, who wrote a series of letters to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i> under the pseudonym "Verax" savaging the institution\'s cleanings. While an 1853 Parliamentary <a href="/wiki/Select_Committee_(Westminster_System)" class="mw-redirect" title="Select Committee (Westminster System)">Select Committee</a> set up to investigate the matter cleared the Gallery of any wrongdoing, criticism of its methods has been erupting sporadically ever since from some in the art establishment.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif/250px-Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif" width="250" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif/375px-Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif/500px-Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif 2x" data-file-width="1998" data-file-height="780" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vignetta-Punch-Restauro.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1847 <i><a href="/wiki/Punch_(magazine)" title="Punch (magazine)">Punch</a></i> cartoon by <a href="/wiki/John_Leech_(caricaturist)" title="John Leech (caricaturist)">John Leech</a> depicting the restoration controversy then ongoing</div></div></div>\n<p>The last major outcry against the use of radical conservation techniques at the National Gallery was seventy-one years ago in the immediate post-war years, following a restoration campaign by Chief Restorer Helmut Ruhemann while the paintings were in Manod Quarry. When the cleaned pictures were exhibited to the public in 1946 there followed a furore with parallels to that of a century earlier. The principal criticism was that the extensive removal of <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnish</a>, which was used in the 19th century to protect the surface of paintings but which darkened and discoloured them with time, may have resulted in the loss of "harmonising" glazes added to the paintings by the artists themselves. The opposition to Ruhemann\'s techniques was led by <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Gombrich" title="Ernst Gombrich">Ernst Gombrich</a>, a professor at the <a href="/wiki/Warburg_Institute" title="Warburg Institute">Warburg Institute</a> who in later correspondence with a restorer described being treated with "offensive superciliousness" by the National Gallery.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> A 1947 commission concluded that no damage had been done in the recent cleanings.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg/250px-George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg" width="250" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg/375px-George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg/500px-George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5935" data-file-height="4226" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:George_Bellows_-_Men_of_the_Docks_-_1912_-_The_National_Gallery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Bellows" title="George Bellows">George Bellows</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Men_of_the_Docks" title="Men of the Docks">Men of the Docks</a></i> (1912)</div></div></div>\n<p>The National Gallery has also been criticised for misattributing paintings. Kenneth Clark\'s decision in 1939 to relabel a group of paintings by anonymous artists of the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> school as works by <a href="/wiki/Giorgione" title="Giorgione">Giorgione</a> (a crowd-pulling artist due to the rarity of his paintings) made him unpopular with his staff. More recently, the attribution of a 17th-century painting of <i>Samson and Delilah</i> (bought in 1980) to Rubens has been contested by a group of art historians, who believe that the National Gallery has not admitted the mistake to avoid embarrassing those who were involved in the purchase, many of whom still work for the Gallery.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In February 2014, <i><a href="/wiki/Men_of_the_Docks" title="Men of the Docks">Men of the Docks</a></i>, by U.S. artist <a href="/wiki/George_Bellows" title="George Bellows">George Bellows</a>, was bought by the National Gallery for $25.5&#160;million (\xc2\xa315.6&#160;million). It was the first major American painting to be purchased by the Gallery. Director <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Penny" title="Nicholas Penny">Nicholas Penny</a> termed the painting a new direction for the Gallery \xe2\x80\x93 a non-European painting in a European style. Its sale was controversial in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_Directors_of_the_National_Gallery">List of Directors of the National Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: List of Directors of the National Gallery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="wikitable" style="margin:auto; margin:0 0 1em 1em;">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Name\n</th>\n<th>Years in office\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lock_Eastlake" title="Charles Lock Eastlake">Charles Lock Eastlake</a></td>\n<td>1855\xe2\x80\x931865\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Boxall" title="William Boxall">William Boxall</a></td>\n<td>1866\xe2\x80\x931874\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/Frederick_William_Burton" class="mw-redirect" title="Frederick William Burton">Frederick William Burton</a></td>\n<td>1874\xe2\x80\x931894\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/Edward_Poynter" title="Edward Poynter">Edward Poynter</a></td>\n<td>1894\xe2\x80\x931904\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Holroyd" title="Charles Holroyd">Charles Holroyd</a></td>\n<td>1906\xe2\x80\x931916\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sir <a href="/wiki/Charles_Holmes" title="Charles Holmes">Charles id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r853264625">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{list-style-type:none;margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>dl>dd{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em;list-style:none}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-100{font-size:100%}</style><div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">\n<dl><dd><b>a.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Anone"><a href="#ref_Anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  Sculptures and applied art are in the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_and_Albert_Museum" title="Victoria and Albert Museum">Victoria and Albert Museum</a>, the British Museum houses earlier art, non-Western art, prints and drawings, and art of a later date is at <a href="/wiki/Tate_Modern" title="Tate Modern">Tate Modern</a>. Some British art is in the National Gallery, but the National Collection of British Art is mainly in <a href="/wiki/Tate_Britain" title="Tate Britain">Tate Britain</a>.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><b>b.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Bnone"><a href="#ref_Bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  St Martin\'s Workhouse (to the east) was cleared for the construction of E. M. Barry\'s extension, whereas <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Barracks,_London" title="St George&#39;s Barracks, London">St George\'s Barracks</a> stayed until 1911, supposedly because of the need for troops to be at hand to quell disturbances in Trafalgar Square. (<a href="#CITEREFConlin2006">Conlin 2006</a>, p.&#160;401) Wilkins hoped for more land to the south, but was denied it as building there would have obscured the view of <a href="/wiki/St_Martin-in-the-Fields" title="St Martin-in-the-Fields">St Martin-in-the-Fields</a>.</dd></dl>\n<dl><dd><b>c.</b> <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Cnone"><a href="#ref_Cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span>  They are as follows: above the main entrance, a blank roundel (originally to feature the <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Wellesley,_1st_Duke_of_Wellington" title="Arthur Wellesley, 1st Duke of Wellington">Duke of Wellington\'s</a> face) flanked by two female figures (personifications of Europe and Asia/India, sites of his campaigns) and high up on the eastern fa\xc3\xa7ade, <a href="/wiki/Minerva" title="Minerva">Minerva</a> by <a href="/wiki/John_Flaxman" title="John Flaxman">John Flaxman</a>, originally <a href="/wiki/Britannia" title="Britannia">Britannia</a>.</dd></dl>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Gallery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em;
